NASA\nBY\n"Many of the technologies of the 1960s and early 1970s\nwere based on prewar science. .\nThere is a big backlog\nof postwar science now maturing. We\'relseeing just the\nleading edge of its commercialization."\n"Technology transfer is a hands-gn process and not a mail\nroom or brochure-type activity.\nThese statements reflect two points that the Advisory Committee and\nthe study team wish to emphasize: first,\nthe time and circumstances are\nripe for action by NASA;second, NASAaction should focus upon one-to-one\ncooperation between its scientists and engineers and those in industry.\nThe following pages summarizethe results of research into NASA\'s\npartnership with industry for transferring technology domestically.\nThe\ndiscussion focuses upon the study\'s point of departure, findings, and\naction recommendations to the Administrator for enhancing technology transfer.\nFrom the outset the study team and the Advisory Committeehave accepted\ntwo fundamental tenets as points of departure for pursuing the task:\n(I) systematic and deliberate programs of technology transfer\npromote and strengthen NASA\'sprimary mission of assuring\nU.S. leadership in the development and application of aeronautical and space science and technology; and,\n(2) an expandedemphasis on technology transfer is worth pursuing for what the U.S. economyin general can realize in\nterms of technical benefits as well as for the potential\nbenefits to NASA.\n3\nClearly, the potential benefits from exploiting technology are large.\nThese benefits can only be realized, however, if the results of research\nand development can be moved successfully from the laboratory to the\neconomy. As a primary supporter of R&Dactivities\nand generator of new\ntechnology, it is essential that the federal government pursue technology\ntransfer activities.\nIwilliam\nApril\n\n18,\n\n2William\nAdvancement\n3See\npecially\n\nF. Miller,\n\n1983,\n\np.\n\nD.\nof\n\nCarey,\n\nExecutive\n\nScience,\n\nAppendix\n\nA,\n\npp.\n\n11-17.\n\n2-6,\n\nPresident, SRI International,\n\nquoted in\n\nBusiness\n\n79.\n\nin\n\nOfficer,\n\nletter\n\nTechnology\n\nto\n\nstudy\n\nInnovation\n\nAmerican\ndirector,\nand\n\nAssociation\nNovember\n\nEconomic\n\nfor\n2,\n\nVitality,\n\nthe\n\n1982.\nes-\n\nWeek,\n\nix\n\nWith or without the government\'s efforts,\ntechnology diffusion will\noccur through traditional\nlines of communication, publication,\njob turnover and so on. The Technology Utilization\nProgram is NASA\'s formal effort\nto supplement this diffusion.\nBy better understanding and capitalizing\non\nthese traditional\nprocesses, NASA\'stechnology transfer efforts can stimulate the diffusion process to accomplishments beyond those of the Technology\nUtilization\nProgram. The avenue explored here to expand technology transfer\nis through enhanced government-industry cooperation.\nWhy should NASA\ntake the initiative\namongfederal agencies in providing leadership on new ways to stimulate government-industry cooperation\nfor the transfer and application of technology? First, NASA\nhad a broader,\nmore extensive technological base (including seven major laboratories and\ntwo flight stations) than any other agency except the Department of Defense.\nSecond, NASA\nhas an explicit legislative\ncharter to pursue technology transfer.\nThird, NASA\nhas the most experience with organized, successful programs\nfor technology transfer.\nThis combination of institutional\nleadership and\nprogram experience, spanning more than 20 years, places NASAin a unique\nposition to take the initiative\nin exploring innovative ways to cooperate\nwith American industry for the more effective exploitation\nof technology. 4\nNotwithstanding the opportunities at hand, and NASA\'scapability to make\nsubstantial progress, the Office of Management nd Budget consistently has\na\nreduced substantially NASA\nrequests to expand its technology transfer activities.\nIt therefore behooves NASA strengthen its efforts to documentand\nto\ndemonstrate the value to NASA\nand to the general economyof technology transfer activities.\nI.\n\nFindings\n\nIn pursuing\nthis exploration,\nthe study team developed\neight working\npapers;\nthe Advisory\nCommittee\nmet on two occasions;\nand members\nof the\nstudy\nteam conducted\nover three dozen\ninterviews\nwith senior\nindustry\nofficials\nregarding\ninnovation\nand technology\ntransfer.\nIn addition,\nthe\nstudy\ndirector\nmade visits\nto assess\nperceptions\nby the Center\nDirectors\nand other officials\nat the seven NASA\nField Centers.\nAs a result,\nthe\nAdvisory\n\nCommittee\n\nand\n\nstudy\n\nteam\n\nreached\n\nthese\n\nfindings:\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Foreign\nhigh technology\ncompeition\nis real\npositive\napproaches,\ne.g., the exploitation\ndomestically;\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Of seven public\npolicy\nareas affecting\npatent,\nantitrust,\nregulatory,\nconflict\n\n4See\nProgram,\n\nAppendix\npp.\n\ni-6.\n\nB,\n\nThe\n\nDevelopment\n\nof NASA\'s\n\nand must\nof U.S.\n\nbe met technology\nat a\nlevel\nof detail\nwhere value versus\nrisk can be well understood.\nWhereas\nin the past applicable\ntechnology\ninnovations\nusually\noccurred\nwithin\na company,\nnow they frequently\noccur outside,\nand cooperative\nefforts\nare required\nto assure\ntimely\nawareness. 6\nNASA can improve\nits understanding\nof industry\'s\nmonitoring\nthe economy\ncompetition,\nand Although each of these areas has the\npotential to inhibit or facilitate\nthe application of technology, current\npolicies in these seven areas generally do not seriously impede increased\nNASAcooperation with industry or the transfer of technology. 7\nHowever, with regard to NASA\npolicy, three areas deserve continued\nevaluation:\npatent policy, antitrust policy, and organizational conflict\nof interest.\nNASA\npolicy basically has favored the private retention of\npatent rights, and a continuation of this stance will facilitate\ntechnology transfer.\nAlthough antitrust policy has not been much of a\nconsideration in past NASA\nendeavors, increasing joint endeavors and\nsimilar cooperative activities\nwill require somecare. 8 Recently the\nJustice Department, with the encouragementof the Department of Commerce,\nappears to be allowing greater flexibility\nfor such arrangements (for\nexample, the Justice Department\'s approval of the Microelectronic and\nComputer Technology Corporation--MCC). On the question of organizational\nconflict of interest, government interaction with private firms that confers benefits on those firms always raises questions about possible conflict\nof interest. 9 The private sector is concerned that no special\nbenefits are provided at public expense which give any one firm a competitive advantage over other firms. NASAshould be sensitive to the issue\nand address it before it becomesa problem rather than later.\nC.\nConditions\nfor the successful\ntransfer\nof technology.\nDiscussions\nwith industry\nexecutives,\nreviews\nof past experience\nwith NASA and other\ntechnology\ntransfer\nprograms,\nand discussions\nwith officials\nat NASA Field\nCenters\ntially\n\nsuggest\nthat there\nfacilitate,\nimprove,\n\nare six conditions\nwhich,\nif met1ocan\nand expand\ntechnology\ntransfer:\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 a clear NASA policy\nregarding\ntransfer--beyond\nthat of the\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nsenior\n\nmanagement\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nthe concept\nand role of technology\nexisting\nTechnology\nUtilization\nProgram;\n\ncommitment,\n\nresources\n\nunderstandding\n\nsupervision,\n\nand\n\napplication\n\nof\n\n;\n\nimproved\n\nsubstan-\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 a\n\ntechnology\n\nand\n\ntransfer\n\nprogram\n\nNASA\nbuilt\n\nand\nupon\n\nnonaerospace\nNASA\'s\n\nindustry;\n\ntechnical\n\nstrengths\n\ncapabilities;\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 emphasis\nand\n\nupon\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 a framework\nassessing\n\n7See\n\nbetween\n\nin\n\nCenter\n\nof objectives,\nand\n\nAppendix\n\nInnovation\n\nField\n\nrewarding\n\nE,\n\nthe\n\nSelected\n\nAmerican\n\n8Ibid.,\n\npp.\npp.\n\ncapabilities\n\nstrategies,\n\nincentives\n\nand\n\nparticipation;\n\nand\n\nmeans\n\nfor\n\nprogress.\n\nGovernmental\n\nEconomy,\n\npp.\n\nPolicies\n\nAffecting\n\nTechnical\n\n44-47.\n\n12-18.\n\n9Ibid.,\n\ntechnical\n\n36-37.\n\n10For\n\nother\n\nnology\nTransfer\npp. I-4.\n\nagency\n\nexperience\n\nExperiences\n\nsee\n\nof other\n\nAppendix\nFederal\n\nF, Lessons\nDepartments\n\nFrom\nand\n\nthe\n\nTech-\n\nAgencies,\n\nxii\nEach of these conditions is addressed below:\nI. Management\nDenver\nResearch\nInstitute\nUniversity\n\nVitality\n\nof\n\nDenver\n\nDivision\n\nA-I\nTECHNOLOGICAL\nINNOVATION\nANDECONOMIC\nVITALITY\nOver the last two decades, certain economists have studied and\nresearched the relationships\nthe pace of technological\neconomic vitality\n\namongU.S. public and private investmentln\n\ninnovation, and the resulting\n\nof the nation.\n\nR&D,\n\nimpacts on the\n\nOneof the most prominent of these econo-\n\nmists has described the significance\n\nof these impacts on national well-being:\n\nThere has been a growing conviction in government and\nelsewhere that the American economyis not growing\nas rapidly as it should, and a growing awareness that\nour rate of economic growth depends very heavily on\nour rate of technical change. Second, the advent and\ncontinuation of the cold war has made it painfully\nobvious that our national security depends on the\noutput of our military research and development\neffort.\nThird, economists and others are coming to\nrealize the full importance in various markets of\ncompetition through newproducts and processes rather\nthan direct price competition.\nFourth, unemployment\ncreated or aggravated by technical change has become\nincreasingly acute, the problem reaching such dimensions that the President recently labeled it one of\nthe foremost problems of the sixties.\nThis observation,\n\nmade18 years ago, remains timely in 1983.\n\nThere is substantial\n\nevidence that the economic problems of the U.S.--\n\ncompetitiveness of our industries\n\nin world trade,\n\ninflation,\n\nand unemployment--are related to the innovation process.\nwill\n\nproductivity,\nThese relationships\n\nbe discussed later.\nFurther evidence exists that the process of technological\n\nnot solely\nsector.\nPolicy,\n\nthe responsibility\n\ninnovation is\n\nof the public sector, nor solely of the private\n\nFrank Press, then Director of the Office of Science and Technology\nimpl_ed that joint\n\npublic-private\n\nefforts\n\nare needed:\n\nA-2\nThe relationship between government and industry\nin promoting the productivity and competitiveness\nof U.S. industry is nowa very important item on\nthis nation\'s agenda. It is evident today that\nthe health of our economyis being adversely affected by a lag in our productivity\nand a decline\nin our industrial innovation. A reversal of this\nsituation is essential, not only for the domestic\neffect, but also to improve our competitive position\nin world markets.\n. .\nWeare already aware of\ncertain conditions that need correction:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 There is insufficient\nincentive on the part\nof industry to innovate boldly--industry\nleaders tell us that it is "safer" to market\nincremental improvements in tried and true\nproducts than to undertake greatly innovative\nR&D.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Industry investment is too low on exploratory\nresearch, particularly\nthat from which the\nresults would be more advantageous to society\nas a whole rather than one firm or industry\nin particular.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Industrial research managers tell us that they\nare having to put a larger share of their incomeinto so-called "defensive" measures to\nmeet new environmental and consumer safety\nstandards--as desirable as these standards may\nbe....\nWemust recognize that they require\nresources that might otherwise be used for more\ninnovative work.\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Equipment and facilities\nare aging and not being replaced as rapidly as necessary to kee_\nU.S. industries productive and competitive. seen_clear:\n\n_i. Because of resource limitations,\nfuture federal efforts\nin the technology transfer area must be more carefully focused\n(i.e., concentrate on maximizing results relative to the amount\nof resources committed).\n2. A re-examination of past and current approaches to federal\ntechnology transfer is required. What lessons do the experiences\nof federal agencies provide that might help today\'s program managers\nstructure their programs and allocate their resources most effectively?\nAny attempt to draw conclusions from the past experiences of federal\nagencies in the technology transfer\n\nfield\n\nrange of experiences is quite varied.\nbeen short-lived,\nstaffing.\n\nThe\n\nA number of agency programs have\n\nand many have suffered from inadequate funding and\n\nStill\n\nothers have achieved successes due largely\n\nof the technology being transferred,\ngroup, the efforts\n\nthe characteristics\n\nof a few highly motivated individuals,\n\nunique factor or situation\napplications.\n\nis fraught with danger.\n\nwhich is unlikely\n\nDespite these limitations,\n\nto the nature\n\nof the client\nor someother\n\nto be duplicated\n\nthe collective\n\nin other\n\nexperiences of\n\nfederal agencies do represent an important source of information.\n\nA\n\nreview of these experiences reveals a number of common\nthemes or "lessons."\nThese lessons are summarizedin capsule form below:\n\ni. The Need to Institutionalize\nTechnology\nTransfer\nActivities--Few\nagencies\nhave succeeded\nin incorporating\ntechnology\ntransfer\nactivities\ninto their basic mission\nand basic programs.\nIn most cases,\nif technology\ntransfer\nactivities\nhave been pursued\nat all, they have been pursued\nas\nan adjunct\nor related\nactivity.\nTransfer\nactivities\nneed to be integrated\nwith\nroutine\nfunctions\n(preparation\nof job descriptions,\ncreation\nof a\nreward\nsystem,\nbudget\npreparation,\nprogram\ngoal setting,\netc.).\nSeparate\nisolated\ntechnology\ntransfer\nstructures\nhave tended\nto be short-lived\nand\noften\nhave had limited\nimpact.\nIn agencies\nwhere\ntransfer\nactivities\nhave been viewed\nas a distraction\nto pursuit\nof the agency\nmission,\nthey\nhave\ntypically\nbeen among the first activities\ncurtailed\nor eliminated\nfor budgetary\nreasons.\nIn addition,\na large portion\nof agency\nactivities\nthat could potentially\nconstitute\ntechnology\ntransfer\n(such as procurement,\ncontracting,\nregulation,\netc.)\nfall outside\nof and are often unrelated\nto\nspecifically\nidentified\nagency\ntransfer\nprograms.\n\nF-3\n2. The Nature of the Client Group and Potential Users--Asency\nsuccesses\nin the technology\ntransfer\nfield appear\nto have been greatly\ninfluenced\nby\nthe characteristics\nof client\ngroups\nand potential\nusers,\nand the nature\nof their\nrelationship\nwith these groups.\nFor example,\nthe Department\nof\nAgriculture\n(USDA) has been quite successful\nin transferring\nagricultural\ntechnology\nto the U.S. farm community.\nThis success\ncan be attributed\nin\npart to the nature\nof the client\ngroup--i.e.,\nfragmented,\nengaged\nin\natomistic\nrather\nthan rivalrous\ncompetition,\nvisible\nand well organized,\nplays\na large role in setting\nagency\nresearch\nand prone\nto experiment\nwith new technology,\n\npriorities,\ngenerally\neducated\netc. 1 In addition,\nUSDA\n\n(through\nits highly\ndecentralized\nsystem\nof county\nagents)\nhas developed\na\nclose\nworking\nrelationship\nwith these potential\nusers\nof new technology.\nThe same high degree\nof success\nwould not be possible\nwith a less cohesive,\nless well defined\nclient\ngroup\nin which\nmembers\nengaged\nin more direct\ncompetition\nwith one another.\nObviously\nthe degree\nof difficulty\nincreases\nwhen\nthe potential\nusers of technology\nare not a part of or related\nto the\nagency\'s\n\ntraditional\n\nclient\n\ngroup(s).\n\n3.\nTransfer\nMechanisms--Agency\ntechnology\ntransfer\nprograms\nhave experimented\nwith a number\nof different\ntransfermechansims\nover the years.\nTraditional\nmechanisms\nsuch as reports,\narticles\nfor technical\njournals,\ncataloguing\nsystems,\nworkshops\nand symposia\nhave been most widely\nused.\nNot surprisingly,\nit is not possible\nto generalize\nas to which mechanism\nis "best"\nor even preferable\nto another.\nThe effectiveness\nof each depends\non the particular\nsituation.\nHowever,\nit does appear\nthat the more passive\nmechanisms\n(basic\ninformation\ncollection\nand dissemination\nthrough\ntraditional\nactive\n\nsources),\nmechanisms\n\nplace)\n\nappear\n\nwhile\nimportant,\ncan play only a\n(where person-to-person\ncontact\n\nto hold\n\ngreater\n\npotential\n\nto\n\nincrease\n\nlimited\nis more\nthe\n\nrole.\nlikely\n\nflow\n\nof\n\nMore\nto take\ntechnology.\n\n4.\nThe Importance\nof Networks\nand Personal\nContact--Federal\nagency\nexperiences\nhighlight\nthe value\nof creating\nnetworks\nand increasing\npersonal\ncontact.\nProximity\nto and regular\ninteraction\nwith potential\nusers\nof\nfederal\ntechnology\nappear to have been important\nelements\nin a number\nof successful\ntransfer\nefforts.\nAlthough\nthere are exceptions,\nde-centralized\nstructures\n(capable\nof promoting\nthis type of interaction)\nappear\nto have\nworked\nbetter\nthan highly\ncentralized\nstructures.\nWhile\nthe evidence\nis\nlimited,\npast experiences\nalso seem to indicate\nthat substantial\nbenefits\ncan result\nfrom joint participation\nprojects\nin which\nthe generators\nand\npotential\nusers\nof technology\nwork together.\n5.\nPolitical\nand Social\nSupport--Past\nexperiences\nclearly\nindicate\nthe need\nfor widespread\npolitical\nand social\nsupport\nfor federal\ntransfer\nactivities\nin order\nfor them to i) succeed\nin achieving\ntheir objectives\nand 2) continue\nto receive\nagency\nsupport\nand funding\nover the long run.\nFew agency\ntechnology\ntransfer\nefforts\nhave enjoyed\nthis type of political\nand social\nsupport.\n6.\nThe Federal\nRole in R&D Support--Federal\nsupport\nof R&D appears\nto have\nbeen most effective\nand least controversial\nwhen strengthening\nthe infrastructure\nof science\nand technology\n(i.e.,\nthrough\neducation,\ntraining,\nbasic\nresearch,\nencountered\nhas\nand\n\nspecific\n\netc.). 2 In general,\nthe level\nincreased\nwhen federal\nsupport\n\nindustries--where\n\neconomic\n\nand\n\nof complexity\nand controversy\nis applied\nto Each, in a different way, provides an example of how technology\nhas been transferred or shared.\nTwo, "Wind Tunnels," and "Crashworthiness Design Technology," are in\naeronautics, while the third, "Goddard Battery Workshop," has its origins\nin a common\nspace technology challenge. Each deals with a program that\nstretches over manyyears.\nThe first,\n"Wind Tunnels," exemplifies a special characteristic\nof\nNASA(and its precurser, NACA)--unique facilities\nand the capability to\nprovide qualified staff for their operation and data reduction.\nThe\ncrashworthiness case study reflects a variety of cooperative means, based\nupon voluntary sharing, to meet common\ntechnical objectives.\nThe battery\nconferences reveal how an ad hoc approach to dealing with a problem became\nregularized into a recognized, annual event.\nThese cases suggest the pervasiveness of opportunities\nsharing and transfer between NASA\nand industry.\n\nfor technology\n\nI-2\nCASE\nSTUDY:WINDTUNNELS\n\n"The wind tunnel is indispensible to the development\nof modern aircraft.\nTodayno aeronautical engineer\nwould contemplate committing an advanced aircraft\ndesign to flight without first measuring its lift and\ndrag properties and its stability\nand controllability\nin a wind tunnel. Tunnel tests first,\nfree]_light\ntests later, is the proper order of things.\nIn 1908, Wilbur Wright startled the Europeanaviation communityby\npiloting his Flyer for a successful one and one-half hour flight.\nWhile\nthe United States government had not purchased any sort of flying machine,\nby this time European countries began to pour major resources into aeronautical development, including wind tunnels. Between 1903 and the start of\nWorld War I, Europe had captured technical leadership in aviation.\nCentralized government-funded aeronautical laboratories were built in England,\nFrance, Germany, Italy and Russia. Whenthe war began, France had 1,400\nmilitary aircraft;\nGermany, 1,000; Russia, 800; and Britian, 400. The U.S.\nflying machine inventory was 23.\nNACA/NASA\'s\n\nResearch\n\nRole\n\nPrior to the end of the war, NACA began drawing\nplans for its\nwind tunnel\nat Langley\nField.\nThe emphasis\nof this new course\nof\nwas based\non the idea that, when the war ended,\nthere would be\n\nfirst\naction\n\nclasses\nof aircraft\nand trained\npersonnel\navailable\nfor full operation.\nAlso envisioned\nwas a strong\nlink to a new industry\nof plane-makers,\nsince\nNACA had no intention\nof manufacturing\naircraft.\nAmes and Lewis Research\nCenters\nboth were placed\nclose\nto the fledgling\naviation\nindustry\nsites in\nrecognition\nfrom it.\n\nof\n\nthe\n\ntwofold\n\npurpose\n\nAirfoil,\nor wing, research\npletion\nof the Variable\nDensity\n\nof\n\nservicing\n\nthe\n\nindustry\n\nand\n\nlearning\n\nbegan as early as 1923 and, with the comTunnel\nat Langley,\nhas become\none of the\n\nmost fruitful\nand long-running\nprograms.\nThe VDT quickly\nestablished\nNACA\nas a technically\ncompetent\nresearch\norganization\nand witnessed\nall manner\nof aircraft\nfrom monstrous\nZeppelins\nto military\naircraft.\nAn NACA Technical\nReport\npuslished\nin 1933 listed\na total of 78 airfoil\nsections\nthat were\ntested.\n\nThis\n\niBaals,\nWashington,\n\ntechnical\n\nDonald\n\nD.,\n\nD.C.:\n\nU.S.\n\nreport\n\nand\n\neventually\n\nCorliss,\n\nGovernment\n\nWilliam\nPrinting\n\nfound\n\nits\n\nway\n\ninto\n\nR. Wind\n\nTunnels\n\nOffice,\n\n1981,\n\nthe\n\nof\npage\n\nNASA,\ni.\n\nI-3\ndesigns of many successful aircraft companies\' ships, e.g., the Douglas\nDC-8 transport, the Boeing B-17 Flying Fortress, and the famous Lockheed\ntwin-tailed P-38. Airfoil\ndesign and analysis remain important areas in\nthe changing designs of manufacturers today. On the west coast, the\noriginal Amestunnel began in 1944and has tested over i00 aircraft.\nNearly anything that flies has been tested at Ames, including sleek jet\nfighters, cargo planes, helicopters, commercial airliners,\nand the\nspace shuttle orbitor.\nCoordinating\n\nArrangements\n\nIn tracing\nprogress\nof aerospace\nand aircraft\nindustries\nover the\npast 65 years,\nit would be impossible\nto ignore\nthe joint efforts\nof\nNACA/NASA\nand private\nindustry.\nEach concern\ninvolved--whether\nit was\n\nthe\n\nAir Force,\nNavy, Lockheed,\nGrumman,\nBell, Rockwell,\nUnited\nTechnology,\nMcDonnell\nDouglas,\nAmes, Langley\nor Lewis--committed\nexpertise,\nproblems,\ntime and money.\nAccording\nto the experience\nof Donald\nBaals,\nan aeronautical\n_ngineer\nwith NACA/NASA\nfo_ more than 40 years,\neach partner\nhad a complementary\nfunction.\nAs the aircraft\nindustry\ndeveloped,\nit was only natural\nthat aircraft\nand NASA Wind\nOffice,\n1981.\n\nof\n\nSciences.\n\nWashington,\n\nD.C.:\n\nDeputy\n\nNASA\'s\n\nRole\n\nNational\n\nDirector\n\nof\n\nAeronautics,\n\nin Aeronautics:\nAcademy\n\nPress,\n\nA Workshop\n1981,\n\npage\n\nv.\n\n1-6\nCASE\nSTUDY: CRASHWORTHINESS TECHNOLOGY\nDESIGN\n\nIn 1972 Langley Research Center in cooperation with the FAAand industry began a program to develop technology for improved crashworthiness\nand occupant survivability\nin general aviation aircraft.\nThe ongoing\nprogram includes formal contract relationships as well as informal exchanges\nof information with the FAA (Research Center in NewJersey and Civil Aeromedical Institute in Oklahoma) and several industrial entities.\nThe effort\nincludes analytical and experimental work and structural concept development.\nCrashworthiness design technology is divided into three areas addressed by\nboth Langley and the FAAbut, due to the nature of each agency\'s designated\nfunctions within the government, their tasks vary.\nUnder the general area of environmental technology, Langley acquires\nactual crash data, through records voluntarily\nsubmitted by air carriers\nand from its own crash tests, to use in prototype development; the FAA, as\na regulatory agency, evaluates quantitative crash data to define a crash\nenvelope. Concerning the second area --airframe design--Langley conducts\nfull scale crash tests, develops and validates advanced analytical techniques,\nand conceives and evaluates concepts for future designs of airframes; the\nFAAassesses current analytical technology. Finally, under componentdesign\ntechnology, Langley develops all aspects of cabin interiors and the FAA\nevaluates seats and restraint systems.\nThe flow of information betweenNASAand the FAAappears to be open and\neasy. Each critiques the other\'s work and joint contracts are sometimes\nlet.\nBoth agencies often participate in the samegovernment, industrial and\nassociation conferences which reinforces each group\'s understanding of the\nother\'s work and needs. There are, however, minor barriers to a totally\nsatisfactory relationship.\nOne official\nat Langley related a perceived "sluggishness" in the setting of regulations at the FAA. Another source related\nthat the Army requires more stringent safety precautions in its helicopters\nthan does the FAA. But, because personnel at Langley work closely with\nboth the FAA, which regulates, and with industry, which bears the expense\nof any regulations, they are careful to "walk a fine line" between them by\nremaining neutral and presenting information only and by not making any recommendations regarding those regulations.\n\nLangley\n\nand\n\nPrivate\n\nResearchers\n\nat\n\nIndustry\nNASA\n\nand\n\nin private\n\nenterprise\n\nstress\n\nthe\n\nimportance\n\nof\n\nworking\nopenly\nwith peers.\nPersonal\ncommunication\nis considered\nimportant\nto\navoid misinterpretations.\nBut in so estoric\nan endeavor\nas flight\nsafety\nwhich\nis based\non mathematics\nand physics,\nit is someL\xc2\xb1m=_\nu_\xc2\xb1eu_L\n_u f_L1u\nothers\nwith enough\nof the same background\nand understanding\nwith whom to\neasily\ncommunicate.\nMost of Langley\'s\nactivities,\ntherefore,\nhave been in\nthe nature\nof sharing\ninformation\nrather\nthan mutual\nparticipation\non particular projects.\nIn many\ninstances\nthere\nis no formal\ncontract\nestablished\nbetween the entities.\nBoth NASA, with its vast facilities\nsuch as wind t,,nnels\n\nI-7\nand laboratories, and industry, with the ability\nto provide test items such\nas seats and other equipment, find it mutually beneficial to foster informal\navenues of cooperation. The following case studies exemplify the results of\nsuch cooperation.\ni.\n\nGrumman\nAerospace Corporation\n\nA close relationship has existed between Grumman\nand Langley for approximately 15 years. Both entities work on crashworthiness but NASAis evidently\nat the forefront of research because Grumman\nhas conducted its work "almost\nas an adjunct to Langley\'s efforts."\nBesides mutually exchanging information,\nGrumman\nhas been a NASAcontractor.\nOne of the better known projects done\nunder NASA\ncontract is DYCAST, computer program which simulates crashes of\na\nplanes, helicopters or even automobiles. The Grumman\nprogram is based on\na Langley concept and was returned to Langley where it was refined and applied\nto vast amounts of crash data in order to further understand impact phenomena.\nIt was also used at Langley to simulate tests of load limiting substructures\n(floors).\nThe benefits to NASA\nand the FAAare in eventual increased aviation\nsafety.\nGrumman\nbenefits by being able to use the program for its own research\nand as a consulting tool which it will market to automobile, helicopter, and\naircraft\ncompanies. DYCAST as prereleased to interested companies and indiw\nviduals for evaluation.\nIn its final form it will be disseminated through\nCOSMIC,and Grumman ill continue to privately sell courses and consulting\nw\non the program\'s specific uses. Personnel at Grumman\nconsider the NASArelationship of prime importance and stated a willingness to give NASA\nprojects\ntop priority\nin task assignments.\n2.\n\nMooneyAircraft\n\nCorporation\n\nMooneyAircraft personnel had been studying laminar flow dynamics.\nWhenit was noticed that there were small inconsistencies between the results\nthey were getting and NASA\'s data, Mooney researchers went to Langley to\nlearn more about the problem. They explained everything they had done and\nNASAagreed to investigate the samearea. Later, Mooney subcontracted to a\nresearch center at Texas A&Mfor further work and no formal contract was\never negotiated with Langley. In relation to the Crashworthiness Program,\nresearchers at Mooney"simply becamefriendly with people at Langley working\nin the samearea." Again there was no particular\nproject, but each visited\nthe other\'s facilities.\nSince the issues involved were so complex, the\ncontact at Mooneyestimates that it took about a year to establish effective\ncommunications between the company\nand Langley.\nSomeof the specific ways in which Mooneyand Langley have shared expertise are: Mooneyused NASAresearch data in air flow research for development of its air foil; Mooneygave NASA\nresults of wind tunnel tests which\nled to insights into prediction of crashworthiness; Mooneywill give full\nscale verification\nof results gained from a new plane on which Mooneyhas\nworked for four years and will soon test.\nThere are no formal contracts\nfor any of these arrangements.\n\nI-8\n3.\n\nBell Helicopter\n\nLangley and Bell "shared and looked at concepts together" to get ideas\nfor civil aircraft.\nFor the last test, Langley used one of Bell\'s experimental seats. The two groups shared all available engineering data. There\nwas no contract involved.\n4.\n\nBoeing, Lockheed and McDonnell Douglas\n\nSometimesa way can be found to transfer information even though one or\nmore of the participants is reluctant.\nSuch was the case when Langley requested crash data from Boeing, Lockheedand McDonnell Douglas for the period\n1959-1979. Aircraft manufacturers are extremely sensitive to the possibility\nof litigation\nover past accidents and none cared to be singled out. Langley\nwas aware of this, and the request was worded so that the companiesknew\nthat only accident trends were to be studied and not specific crashes or\nspecific carriers.\nThe companiescomplied, went through their own files to\npreserve confidentiality,\nand were entitled to all information derived from\nthe study.\n5.\n\nFord Motor Company\n\nAlthough not part of the Crashworthiness Program, another type of\n"partnership" is exemplified by NASA\nand Ford. Ford Motor Company\nprovided\ntest vehicles for noise reduction studies and was given test data to further\nits own research.\nConferences\nLangley\nResearch\nCenter\nactively\nparticipates\nin industrial\nand association conferences\nand, of course,\nholds many seminars\nand workshops\nof its\nown.\nAt the workshops,\nsmall groups\nof research\npeers\nare invited\nto attend\nand interact.\nAll participants\nseem to freely\nshare their most current\ndata.\nA formal\nconference\nwas recently\nheld jointly\nby\n(American\nInstitute\nof Aeronautics\nand Astronautics).\n\nNASA and the AIAA\nThe attendees\nwere\n\nprincipally\nmembers\nof nonaerospace\nindustries\nfrom the Fortune\ni000.\nSpeakers\nwere from Langley,\nLewis, Ames and Headquarters.\nThe topic was\nAdvanced\nMaterials\nTechnologies.\nParticipants\nwere encouraged\nto interact\nwith\nthe agency\nrepresentatives\nand among themselves.\n"Partnership"\narrangements\nare expected\nto spinoff\nfrom these meetings.\nThe Society\nof Automotive\nEngineers\n(SAE) has provided\na well respected\nforum\nfor the presentation\nof Langley\npapers\n(as well as others)\nand for the\nexchange\nof information\namong all researchers\ninterested\nin aviation.\nLangley\nreports\nmuch of its seat and substructure\nwork here.\nThe EAA (Experimental\nAircraft\nAssociation),\ndirected\nby Paul Poberezny,\nis the largest\nexperimental\naviation\ngroup\nin existence.\nThe purpose\nof its\nmeetings\nis the open ended transfer\nof technology.\nAttendees\ninclude\nresearchers, manufacturers,\nand government\nagency\npersonnel.\n\nI-9\nNASA\n\nand\n\nthe\n\nRadio\n\nTechnical\n\nCommission\n\nfor\n\nAeronautics\n\n(RTCA)\n\nThe RTCA is a nongovernment\norganization\nlooking\nat problems\nin aviation.\nIt is funded\nby the FAA.\nLangley\nis assisting\nthe RTCA by studying\nin depth\na problem\nwith the reliability\nof emergency\nlocator\ntransmitters.\nInformation is being provided\nby every\nsegment\nof the aviation\ncommunity.\nThe\ninvestigating\ncommittee,\ncalled\nSpecial\nCommittee\n- 136, is chaired\nby\nDr. Robert\nThomson\nof Langley.\nLangley\nis demonstrating\nELT sensor\nactivation problems\nby mounting\nELT specimens\nin full-scale\ncrash test aircraft\nas well as conducting\nother\ntests.\nOther members\nof RTCA will provide\ndata\nfrom actual\ncrashes\nand non-distress\nactivation.\n\nBarriers\n\nand\n\nIncentives\n\nVery few barriers\nmade for this report.\ncerned\nin being able\n\nto cooperative\nefforts\nare evident\nfrom the interviews\nContacts\nfrequently\nstressed\nthe benefits\nto all conto openly\ndiscuss\nand share data with others\nin the field\n\nof crash\ndynamics.\nThere do not seem to be many problems\nwith proprietary\ninformation\nsince,\nin one contact\'s\nwords,\n"What happens\nis that details\nof specific\nresearch\nare sufficiently\ncomplex\nthat only researchers\non the\nproblem\nat the moment\nare able to understand\nit.\nDetails\nof the mathematics\nand testing\nare so precise\nthat, unless\none has been working\nfor some time\non it, the information\nwould not be useful.\nAnyone\nelse might as well wait\nfor the information\nto be published."\nHowever,\nthe esoteric\nnature\nof the\ntechnology\nmay make current\nmethods\nof scientific\nexchange\nless than efficient.\nIn so highly\ntechnical\na field as crash dynamics,\nresearchers\nmust often\nspend a great deal of time searching\nfor other\nindividuals\nworking\non exactly\nthe same problem\nand using\nsimilar\napproaches.\nIn general,\nthough,\nit was\nfelt that an imposed\nsystematized\nmethod\nof technology\nand\nneeds\nto work more closely\nwith their research\npeople\nto better\nunderstand\nthe intricacies\ninvolved.\nPerhaps\na more concrete\nbarrier\nis industry\'s\nfear of litigation.\nAircraft\nmanufacturers,\nfor instance,\nfear regulations\nmade by the FAA but they\nare also reluctant\nto make changes\nthrough\ntheir own research\nbecause\nthey\nmay be held responsible\nfor not making\nthem sooner,\nespecially\nif it could\nbe shown\nthat injury\noccurred\nbecause\nof lack of innovation.\nConsequently\nthey must be dealt with sensitively\nby NASA.\n\nReferences\nHayduk,\nRobert\nJ., Robert\nG. two-day\nreview\nwas held at Goddard\nSpace Flight\nCenter\nto\ndetermine\nwhy cells in the battery\nof an orbiting\nastronomical\nlaboratory\nhad failed\na performance\ntest.\nThose involved\nwere battery\nusers\nfrom\nindustry\nand government\nand the manufacturer\nof the cells\nin question.\nTwo things\nbecame\napparent:\nfirst,\nthat the manufacturer\nwas reluctant\nto share\nproprietary\nproduction\ninformation\nand, second,\nthat attendees\nhad not previously\nhad such an opportunity\nto communicate\nwith each other.\nTo alleviate\nthe latter\nproblem\nand to increase\nefficiency\nand lifetime\nof\nspacecraft\nbattery\nsystems\nthrough\nexchange\nof information\nbetween\nusers\nand manufacturers,\nthe first formal\nannual\nBattery\nWorkshop\nwas held at\nGoddard\nin 1969.\nEach year the workshop\nis announced\nin publications\nsuch as Advanced\nBattery\nTechnolosy\nand Batteries\nToday.\nInvitations\nare sent to the\nElectrochemical\nSociety\nand a mailing\nlist of 600 interested\npeople\namassed\nover the years.\nParticipation\nis open to all including\nbattery\nusers,\nmanufacturers,\nand researchers\nfrom industry,\ngovernment,\nand academia;\nforeigners must have clearance\nto attend.\nAt the 1981 workshop\n222 individuals\nattended\nfrom the following\ntypes of organizations:\n24 different\nbattery\nmanufacturers,\n22 battery\nusers,\n17 government\nand military\nagencies,\n11\nuniversities\nand consulting\nfirms,\nand 25 other types.\nThe workshop\nis oriented\nto emphasize\nproblem\nsolving\nand getting\nresults.\nUnlike\na conference\nor symposium,\nthe intent\nis not to make formal\npresentations\nof dated material\nbut to bring notes\nand visuals\ndescribing\nup-to-theminute\nprogress.\nFree-wheeling\ndiscussion\nand intercommunication\nare considered\nof prime\nimportance.\nFailures\nand problems\nare verbally\ncommunicated\nwhich\nwould\nnot have been admitted\nto in formal\npapers.\nOriginally,\ncussion\nsessions\n\nthe number\nwas not.\n\nof presentations\nwas limited\nNASA used a court reporting\n\nand length\nof disservice\nto provide\n\nverbatim\ntranscripts\nof the sessions.\nHowever,\nmore and more people\nrequested\ntime to give reports;\nsome had to present\na paper or they couldn\'t\ncome.\nTo allow\nfor this and yet not curtail\ndiscussion\ntime, the workshop\nwas lengthened\nfrom two to three days around\n1973.\nAt about\nthe same time\nNASA started\nproducing\nthe proceedings\nas a conference\npublication,\nwhich\nare available\nthrough\nScientific\nTechnical\nAerospace\nReports\n(STAR)\nand\nSelected\nCurrent\nAerospace\nNotices\n(SCAN).\nThe first workshop\nbatteries\nwere\nincluded\nTime \' -_\nformat\nhas\n\nbeen\n\ntopic was nickel\ncadmium\nbatteries;\nnickel\nin the mid-1970\'s\nand lithium\nin the late\n\n_-_ ..... ___\naltered\nto\n\n....\noptimally\n\nand prepared\npresentations.\nsessions,\npaper\npresentations,\n\n_\n\n_^_ ......\n_\naccomodate\nboth\n\nThe workshop\nis now\nand question/answer\n\n_ .... +k_\nfreewheeling\n\na combination\nperiods.\n\nof\n\nhydrogen\n1970\'s.\nyears\n+_\ndiscussion\npanel\n\n1-12\nIt normally costs $25,000 to put on a workshop, including a court reporter at $8,000-$10,000 taping, word processing, typing, reviewing, etc.\nFunding for the 1982 workshop was cut to $7,000-$8,000.\nThe workshop is unique in the industry.\nAn Electrochemical Society\nconference requires reports to be submitted 6-9 months before presentation.\nBecause of the workshop\'s informal format and no prior clearance requirement,\na person can showup on the day of a session and present yesterday\'s results.\nQuality of presentations and visuals may not be uniformly high due to the\nlack of screening, but this drawback is more than compensatedfor by the\ncatalytic stimulation of ideas.\nAs an annual affair,\nthe workshop has established a feeling of continuity\nand comaraderie throughout the industry.\nThe pattern of communication and\ninformation sharing that has been produced otherwise wouldn\'t exist.\nWhile\nyear-round communication within the industry has been enhanced by contacts\nmadeat the workshops, what occurs at a workshop cannot be duplicated.\nThe\nvariety of ideas presented within those three days brings the overall picture\nof a development\'s status into focus in a way two people can never approach.\nThe feedback a manufacturer gets en masseat a workshop may be considered\nmore valuable than individual customer =o_nplaints.\n\nParticipants\nThe workshop\nindustry.\nThey\nproducts,\n\nis directed\nby the battery\nusers,\ncandidly\ndescribe\ntheir experiences\n\nincluding\n\ntest\n\nresults\n\nas well\n\nas\n\nactual\n\nfrom both\nwith the\nperformance\n\ngovernment\nand\nmanufacturers\'\nsuccesses\n\nand\n\nfailures.\nManufacturers\nbasically\nlisten\nto this feedback,\ntaking\nthe\nproblems\nraised\nback to their own laboratories\nto examine\nin greater\ndetail.\nWhile\ndiscussion\nof performance\ncan be very specific--for\nexample,\npinpointing\nexactly\nwhere a leak occurs\nin a certain\nbattery\nand under what\nconditions--the\ndiscussion\ndoes not go into production\nmethods\nthat may have\nresulted\nin the leak, as such methods\nare considered\nproprietary\nby the manufacturer.\nUsers\'\ncomments\nare limited\nto government\nprograms\nfor which\ntest\nresults\nare available\nand public\ninformation;\ntesting\nfor the commercial\nsector\n\nis
